<span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D1538-3881%2F160%2F5%2F223\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">1538-3881/160/5/223</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>Strong gravitational lenses are a rare and instructive type of astronomical object. Identification has long relied on serendipity, but different strategies\u2014such as mixed spectroscopy of multiple galaxies along the line of sight, machine-learning algorithms, and citizen science\u2014have been employed to identify these objects as new imaging surveys become available. We report on the comparison between spectroscopic, machine-learning, and citizen-science identification of galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates from independently constructed lens catalogs in the common survey area of the equatorial fields of the Galaxy and Mass Assembly survey. In these, we have the opportunity to compare high completeness spectroscopic identifications against high-fidelity imaging from the Kilo Degree Survey used for both machine-learning and citizen-science lens searches. We find that the three methods\u2014spectroscopy, machine learning, and citizen science\u2014identify 47, 47, and 13 candidates, respectively, in the 180 square degrees surveyed. These identifications barely overlap, with only two identified by both citizen science and machine learning. We have traced this discrepancy to inherent differences in the selection functions of each of the three methods, either within their parent samples (i.e., citizen science focuses on low redshift) or inherent to the method (i.e., machine learning is limited by its training sample and prefers well-separated features, while spectroscopy requires sufficient flux from lensed features to lie within the fiber). These differences manifest as separate samples in estimated Einstein radius, lens stellar mass, and lens redshift. The combined sample implies a lens candidate sky density of ~0.59 deg<SUP>\u22122</SUP> and can inform the construction of a training set spanning a wider mass\u2013redshift space. A combined approach and refinement of automated searches would result in a more complete sample of galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates for future surveys.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=1538-3881/160/5/223&doi=10.3847/1538-3881/abb612&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=1538-3881/160/5/223&doi=10.3847/1538-3881/abb612&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2020AJ....160..223K\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/1538-3881/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612s1\" name=\"ajabb612s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Elliptical galaxies' structure, kinematics, and formation histories are a compelling test of the Cold Dark Matter (\u039bCDM) paradigm, as they are the end product of galaxy formation (De Lucia et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib30\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib30\">2006</a>). Furthermore, those that act as strong gravitational lenses appear in every respect to be just like other elliptical galaxies, so results of their study can therefore be generalized to all spheroidal galaxies in the observed mass range (Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib4\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib4\">2009</a>). These lensing systems provide a highly accurate measurement of the <em>total</em> mass inside the Einstein radius, and therefore the dark matter content, in these elliptical galaxies that compares well with current galaxy evolution models and assumptions (Shu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib65\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib65\">2015</a>, and reference therein). To date, gravitational lenses have proven General Relativity to be correct with high accuracy over galaxy-wide scales (Collett et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib22\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib22\">2018</a>) and may provide an excellent test case for other theories of gravity, such as the Emergent Gravity recently proposed by Verlinde (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib77\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib77\">2017</a>), through a combination of lensing and kinematic measurements (see Tortora et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib72\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib72\">2018</a>).</p><p>Gravitational lensing has been a powerful technique to measure the masses of the <em>most massive</em> elliptical galaxies (Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib37\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib37\">2007</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib11\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib11\">2008a</a>), as well as helping to understand their Fundamental Plane (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib15\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib15\">2008b</a>) and the stellar population's mass-to-light ratio, and therefore their initial mass function (IMF; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib4\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib4\">2009</a>; Hopkins <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib44\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib44\">2018</a>). The observational drive is now to measure their mass content throughout the spheroidal galaxy mass function (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib12\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib12\">2006</a>), explore the Fundamental Plane in different environments (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib74\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib74\">2009</a>), constrain the stellar mass-to-light ratio in nearby ellipticals (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib75\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib75\">2006</a>; Collier et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib23\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib23\">2018a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib24\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib24\">2018b</a>), discover dark matter substructure in known strong galaxy lens cases (Vegetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib76\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib76\">2012</a>; Cyr-Racine et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib25\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib25\">2019</a>), and independently measure <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> through time-delay cosmography (e.g., H0LiCOW, Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib69\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib69\">2017</a>; Chen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib20\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib20\">2019</a>). These observational studies need larger samples of lenses. Only with a significant expansion of the lensing sample can the effects of evolution and stellar mass be decoupled (e.g., Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib75\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib75\">2006</a>).</p><p>Thus far, lensing arcs have predominantly been identified in <em>massive</em> (&gt;\u202f10<sup>11</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) lens systems thanks to selection biases: SDSS spectroscopic targets are volume-weighted to greater mass (intrinsically bright galaxies are included over a greater volume), and visual identification favors well-separated arcs and lenses (Shu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib65\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib65\">2015</a>). With the Galaxy and Mass Assembly survey (GAMA) spectroscopy-selected sample (Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>), a greater range in lens masses is now available. However, the drive for much larger samples has led to increased searches using machine learning to constrain \u039bCDM in detail (Petrillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib61\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib61\">2019b</a>; Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib47\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib47\">2019</a>; Speagle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib68\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib68\">2019</a>; Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib45\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib45\">2020a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib46\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib46\">2020b</a>; Li et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>).</p><p>The success and completeness of the different identification techniques are difficult to test against one another. This has motivated our study here that benefits from three independent identifications\u2014spectroscopic, machine-learning, and citizen-science\u2014on the same target fields. Our aim is to compare all three techniques to map out an optimal path for future searches. In the following calculations, we adopt a flat \u039bCDM cosmological model with <em>h</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.738, where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn1.gif\" alt=\"${h}_{0}=\\tfrac{{H}_{0}}{100}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{h}_{0}=\\tfrac{{H}_{0}}{100}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> as indicated in Riess et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib62\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib62\">2011</a>), and \u03a9<sub><em>m</em>,0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.262.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612s2\" name=\"ajabb612s2\">2.\u00a0Identifying Strong Galaxy\u2013Galaxy Lenses</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The principal selection technique for galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses has been the identification of double spectral profiles in a single aperture based spectrum (blended spectra, see Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib13\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib13\">2004</a>; Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>; R. L. Steele et al. 2020, in preparation). Searches for blended spectra in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) have been highly successful in identifying strong lens candidate galaxies by identifying spectra containing both a low-redshift passive galaxy and emission lines from a much higher redshift lensed source. In order to confirm such sources as true lensing systems, one requires significantly higher spatial resolution imaging than SDSS can provide.</p><p>Eighty-five cases of strong lenses have already been confirmed with Hubble Space Telescope (HST) imaging through the Sloan Lens ACS survey (SLACS; Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib50\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib50\">2006</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib75\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib75\">2006</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib37\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib37\">2007</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib11\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib11\">2008a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib15\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib15\">2008b</a>; Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib36\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib36\">2008</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib4\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib4\">2009</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib74\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib74\">2009</a>). The SLACS survey was efficient at finding these rare strong lensing objects: approximately 50% of the blended spectra (with a dispersion estimate of the Einstein radius) targeted were confirmed with HST observations, and its success has expanded into the BOSS survey and higher redshifts (BELLS, 0.4\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.7, Brownstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib16\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib16\">2012</a>; Shu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib64\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib64\">2016</a>).</p><p>However, due to the depth and completeness limitations of SDSS spectroscopy\u2014the main survey depth is <em>m</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a017.7 (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib34\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib34\">2001</a>)<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#ajabb612fn1\">8</a></sup>\n\u2014typically only massive foreground galaxy lenses can be identified (10<sup>11</sup>\u201310<sup>12</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, SDSS-Luminous Red Galaxy (LRG) sample, Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib34\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib34\">2001</a>) at intermediate redshifts (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.05\u20130.5). According to Hilbert et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib41\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib41\">2008</a>) the lensing cross section drops rapidly below <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.5, so naturally there are fewer lenses at lower redshifts. However, Sonnenfeld et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib67\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib67\">2015</a>), referencing Arneson et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib3\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib3\">2012</a>) and Gavazzi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib35\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib35\">2014</a>), points to the Einstein radius as the main quantity determining the detection probability as opposed to the lensing cross section. Lens candidate identification through any spectroscopic method requires sufficient flux from the background galaxy in order to obtain a second spectral match. For lensing systems whose Einstein radius exceeds the radius of the instrument's aperture (1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 radius for SDSS), the probability of detection goes down significantly and rapidly (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib67\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib67\">2015</a>, and reference therein).</p><h3 id=\"ajabb612s2-1\" name=\"ajabb612s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0GAMA Spectroscopic Identification</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The GAMA survey (Driver et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib33\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib33\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib32\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib32\">2011</a>) is a multiwavelength survey built around a deep and highly complete redshift survey of five fields with the Anglo-Australian Telescope. GAMA has three major advantages over SDSS in the identification of blended spectra: (1) the spectroscopic limiting depth is 2 magnitudes deeper (<em>m</em><sub><em>r</em></sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a019.8 mag.), (2) the completeness is close to 98% (Liske et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib56\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib56\">2015</a>), and (3) the <span class=\"small-caps\">autoz</span> redshift algorithm easily identifies spectra with signal from two different redshifts (Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib6\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib6\">2014</a>).</p><p>In the GAMA survey, Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>) identified 104 strong lensing candidates from their blended spectra, all of which showed a passive galaxy (PG) with an emission line galaxy (ELG) at higher redshift. Chan et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib19\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib19\">2016</a>) found 10 of a subset of 14 of these spectroscopically identified lens candidates to be probable lenses using deep Subaru imaging. This GAMA spectroscopy sample is dominated by lower mass spheroidal galaxies and higher redshift galaxies (R. L. Steele et al. 2020, in preparation). These GAMA strong lens candidates from Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>) and R. L. Steele et al. (2020, in preparation) extend the stellar mass range and provide a medium-redshift observation in between the SLACS, S4TM, and BELLS samples.</p><p>As mentioned above, the spectroscopic approach is intrinsically limited by the aperture of the spectroscopy: if the lensed features fall outside it, the signal of the lensed (source) galaxy will be weak and unlikely to be detected (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib67\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib67\">2015</a>, and reference therein). As a result of this, GAMA spectroscopy's 1'' radius aperture structurally misses lower- redshift and higher mass lens candidates. This point is discussed further in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s4-2\">4.2</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s6-3\">6.3</a> in relation to other identified candidates. GAMA has improved the spectroscopic identification of lower mass strong lenses, but a much larger sample of lower redshift (<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.1) lenses is needed to constrain mass-to-light ratios in ellipticals.</p></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s2-2\" name=\"ajabb612s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Beyond Spectroscopic Identification of Lenses</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><h4 id=\"ajabb612s2-2-1\" name=\"ajabb612s2-2-1\">2.2.1.\u00a0Machine-learning Identification of Lenses</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Machine learning is gaining popularity as a method for identifying galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates, e.g., in Subaru Hyper-Supreme Cam (Speagle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib68\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib68\">2019</a>), DECAM (Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib46\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib46\">2020b</a>), and Dark Energy Survey data (Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib47\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib47\">2019</a>). Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib61\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib61\">2019b</a>) introduced and developed a machine-learning technique to visually identify strong lens candidates by training the convolutional neural networks to recognize the characteristic arcs that appear next to a lensing elliptical galaxy using simulated images as their training set. These \"mock lenses\" are created by simulating lens features around the images of real galaxies that are selected using color\u2013magnitude criteria modified from SDSS-LRG selection (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib34\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib34\">2001</a>) and with the Einstein radius parameter drawn from a logarithmic distribution in the range of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />0\u20135<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />0, intentionally resembling SLACS lenses. Their selection process included a follow-up visual inspection of each candidate by seven members of the team, which provided a score for each candidate between 0 (reflecting low confidence) and 70 (reflecting high confidence). This new identification method resulted in the ~1300 candidates of the galaxy\u2013galaxy Lenses in the Kilo Degree Survey (LinKS) sample (KiDS; de Jong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib29\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib29\">2013</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib28\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib28\">2015</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib27\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib27\">2017</a>; Kuijken et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib51\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib51\">2019</a>), which overlaps with 100% of the equatorial fields of the GAMA survey (fields G09, G12, and G15). These identifications by Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>) have shown that there are many more strong lenses to be found in the same survey area using identification methods other than spectroscopic. This approach has succeeded in finding more candidate strong lenses similar to the simulated massive elliptical galaxies on which the neural network was trained, i.e., large ellipticals (LRGs) with characteristics similar to those of SLACS lens candidates.</p></div><h4 id=\"ajabb612s2-2-2\" name=\"ajabb612s2-2-2\">2.2.2.\u00a0Citizen-science Identification of Lenses</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>GalaxyZoo (Lintott et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib55\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib55\">2008</a>; Marshall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib57\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib57\">2016</a>) has classified KiDS postage stamps of galaxies on the same area that corresponds to the GAMA/KiDS survey overlap (Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib43\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib43\">2019</a>; L. S. Kelvin et al. 2020, in preparation) using a question tree design shown below in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f1\">1</a>. It leads participants through a series of questions that (given the object is a galaxy) will arrive at a question that prompts them to identify any number of the following \"odd features\" in the image: \"None,\" \"Ring,\" \"Lens or arc,\" \"Dust lane,\" \"Irregular,\" \"Other,\" and \"Overlapping.\" The GalaxyZoo team imposed a redshift restriction of <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.15 in the pre-selection of images to be classified by participants. With the voting completed on the GAMA/KiDS equatorial fields, we can now analyze the results of this citizen-science approach.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0The question tree employed by GalaxyZoo citizen science, which volunteer participants use to classify images from the Kilo Degree Survey (KiDS). Second Tier Question T06 prompts participants to identify \"odd features,\" of which an option is \"Lens or arc.\" Participants can choose more than one option in T06. We used results from this question to determine the final cut for our GalaxyZoo sample selection.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f1\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f1\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>With three independent techniques to identify strong gravitational lenses in the same three fields, we have an opportunity to test how well different identification techniques can be calibrated against one another, discover implicit selection effects in each, and approximate the on-sky density of strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates.</p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612s3\" name=\"ajabb612s3\">3.\u00a0Data and Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In order to obtain a valid comparison of the three techniques, the catalogs of candidate lenses obtained by GAMA blended spectra (Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>), LinKS machine learning (Petrillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>), and GalaxyZoo citizen science (L. S. Kelvin et al. 2020, in preparation) were cut to represent only those candidates identified within the equatorial GAMA fields G09, G12, and G15, which is the area of overlap between GAMA and KiDS. We show the presence of candidates identified by each method in each of those fields in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f2\">2</a>. This cut resulted in usable catalogs consisting of 85 spectroscopically identified lens candidates, 421 candidates identified by LinKS machine learning, and a misleading total of 12,934 GalaxyZoo candidates with \"Lens or arc\" scores of 0 or higher. Further cuts (see below in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-1\">3.1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-2\">3.2</a>) to the catalogs were then made to account for false positives in each of the samples.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0The three GAMA/KiDS fields (G09, G12, and G15) within which data from all three lensing identification techniques are available. GAMA spectroscopic, LinKS machine-learning, and GalaxyZoo citizen-science galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates are identified in all three fields. The candidates shown here are those that passed final selection criteria.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f2\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f2\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Stellar mass estimates and redshifts for each candidate were taken from the GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> photometric catalog (Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib80\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib80\">2016</a>), converted to stellar masses using the prescription from Taylor et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib71\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib71\">2011</a>) from GAMA DR2/3 (Liske et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib56\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib56\">2015</a>; Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib8\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib8\">2018</a>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#ajabb612fn2\">9</a></sup>\n We note that the stellar masses from the M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> (Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib79\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib79\">2018</a>)<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#ajabb612fn3\">10</a></sup>\n catalog are a factor of 2 smaller than the <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> photometric estimates (Taylor et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib70\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib70\">2020</a>). Both the M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> and <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> photometry stellar masses are based on the same <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> photometry. The documentation notes that both are not corrected for aperture using the S\u00e9rsic fits from Kelvin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib48\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib48\">2012</a>) because <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> uses matched apertures. The M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> is a full spectral energy distribution (SED) treatment from the ultraviolet to the submillimeter, and <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> stellar mass estimates use <em>u</em>\u2013<em>Y</em> photometry. Both use Chabrier IMFs and the Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib17\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib17\">2003</a>) stellar template models. The M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> uses a mix of dust models, while the <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> mass catalog uses a screen with the Calzetti &amp; Heckman (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib18\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib18\">1999</a>) extinction law. Scatter between stellar mass estimates from photometry using <em>M/L</em> ratio prescriptions is typically 0.3 dex (see, e.g., Cluver et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib21\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib21\">2014</a> and Kettlety et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib49\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib49\">2018</a> for the discrepancy between M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> and WISE photometry mass estimates). Given that both are based on the same photometry, without aperture corrections, and that M<span class=\"small-caps\">ag</span>P<span class=\"small-caps\">hys</span> was developed with star-forming galaxies in mind and the <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> mass catalog was calibrated for ellipticals (also see Taylor et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib70\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib70\">2020</a>), we opt for the <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> mass catalog as the most appropriate for our sources. Candidates were matched between independent catalogs by GAMA ID, except for the LinKS sample, which was matched based on R.A. and Decl.</p><h3 id=\"ajabb612s3-1\" name=\"ajabb612s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0GAMA Blended Spectra and LinKS Machine-learning Catalogs</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The lens candidate sample provided by Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>) is based on spectral match, and therefore does not include a subjective follow-up visual inspection. However, in order to attain a more pure sample for consideration here, we selected candidates with a minimum difference of \u0394<em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.1 between the redshift of the PG spectral match and an ELG match at higher redshift. This selection is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f3\">3</a> in comparison with Grade-A SLACS lenses from Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib4\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib4\">2009</a>). The result of this selection is 47 candidates identified by GAMA spectroscopy, 31 of which have stellar mass estimates from the GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> catalog.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Selection of GAMA spectroscopy candidates based on redshift difference between lens and source. Blue markers denote GAMA spectroscopy candidates from Holwerda et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>). In all cases, the passive galaxy match is at lower redshift than the emission line galaxy match. The shaded area represents redshift differences greater than 0.1, which indicate likely lens candidates. We remove all GAMA spectroscopy candidates outside this shaded region, which are more likely interacting or close paired galaxies. Red markers show redshift difference for SLACS candidates taken from Auger et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib4\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib4\">2009</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f3\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f3\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The LinKS machine-learning catalog of 421 candidates obtained from Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>) included all those objects whose visual inspection score was greater than 0. In order to compare only the highest quality candidates, a score threshold of &gt;17 was taken from Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>). This reduced the LinKS machine-learning catalog to 47, of which 46 have stellar mass estimates in the <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> catalog. All following mentions of the GAMA spectroscopy and LinKS machine-learning samples refer to these reduced selections of candidates unless otherwise specified.</p></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s3-2\" name=\"ajabb612s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0GalaxyZoo Citizen-science Catalog</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>GalaxyZoo presented the most variables to consider when analyzing its reliability as a method for identifying strong lens candidates. The percentage of votes for lensing features in each candidate is taken to be a subjective score given in a similar manner to the LinKS machine-learning catalog. However, since the program was not designed specifically to identify only lenses, each candidate's score must also be considered relative to scores of other choices within the same question level. For each candidate in question, other \"odd features\" could potentially pull votes away from the \"Lens or arc\" classification. This aspect is specific to GalaxyZoo citizen science among the three methods considered here: GalaxyZoo simultaneously considers a range of other classification results that add false positives and noise to the data when attempting to focus on one type of object, e.g., strong lens candidates. All other classifications must be accounted for in the cleaning of the GalaxyZoo sample.</p><p>Selection votes could be distributed unpredictably across the seven options, so we took a multifaceted selection informed by visual comparison of candidates of a range of \"Lens or arc\" scores, examples of which are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f4\">4</a>, as well as comparing them with LinKS machine-learning candidates, in addition to analyzing ratios of \"Lens or arc\" scores to the scores given to each of the other six \"odd feature\" options, which we demonstrate in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f5\">5</a>. Our final selection considered only those candidates with the following characteristics:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">\"Lens or Arc\" score was greater than all other \"odd feature\" options' scores.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">\"Lens or Arc\" score was greater than 30%.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Thin-lens Einstein radius estimate (Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s4-1\">4.1</a> for details) is larger than the point-spread function (PSF) for KiDS imaging (0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />65).</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Examples of lens candidates identified by GalaxyZoo citizen science, taken from KiDS (<em>g-, r-,</em> and <em>i</em>-bands). G593809 (top left) represents a \"Lens or arc\" score of ~10% of votes. G123523 (top right) represents ~20%. G177638 (middle left) represents ~25%. G505441 (middle right) represents ~30%. G372045 (bottom left) represents ~35%. G324764 (bottom right) represents ~40%. These are representative of the images that would be viewed by GalaxyZoo participants, as well as informing our final cutoff for the GalaxyZoo selection considered here.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f4\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f4\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Ratios of the fractions of \"Lens or arc\" selections on the vertical axis with respect to the fractions of selections of the other six \"odd features\" options from GalaxyZoo Question Tree question T06, shown above in Figure <a href=\"#ajabb612f1\">1</a>, on the horizontal axis. Solid lines indicate boundaries used in the final cut for the GalazyZoo selection. Only points that fall within the shaded region of all six plots are selected for our final GalaxyZoo sample.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f5\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f5\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>This final cut reduced the GalaxyZoo citizen science catalog to 13 candidates (listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#ajabb612t1\">1</a>) of comparable reliability to the other two techniques, according to our own visual inspection of various scoring lens candidates. These 13 candidates all have reliable stellar mass estimates from the GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> mass catalog. All following mentions of the GalaxyZoo citizen-science sample refer to this reduced selection of candidates unless otherwise specified.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"ajabb612t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nGalaxyZoo Citizen-science Lens Candidates\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612t1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/suppdata/ajabb612t1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">GAMA ID</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Lens Score</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn11.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })</script></span></span>\n</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>z</em></th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>\u03b8</em><sub>E,TL</sub> (arcsec)</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>\u03b8</em><sub>E,SIS</sub> (arcsec)</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R.A. align=\"center\">2.13</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/suppdata/ajabb612t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-3881/abb612\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/suppdata/ajabb612t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s3-3\" name=\"ajabb612s3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Einstein Radius Estimation</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Limited by the exclusion of velocity dispersion measurements of the GAMA/KiDS fields, we estimate the Einstein radius for each candidate based on empirical fits of total stellar mass <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> to lensing parameters <em>M</em><sub>E</sub> (the total mass enclosed within the Einstein radius) and <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub> (velocity dispersion).</p><h4 id=\"ajabb612s3-3-1\" name=\"ajabb612s3-3-1\">3.3.1.\u00a0Thin-lens Estimate</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We approximate the lens galaxy as a thin-lens system with the lens and source positioned along the same line of sight, where the Einstein radius (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>) is given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}={\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{{\\rm{E}}}}{{10}^{8.09}{M}_{\\odot }}\\right)}^{1/2}{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{{LS}}}{{D}_{L}{D}_{S}}\\mathrm{Mpc}\\right)}^{1/2}\\ {\\rm{arcsec}}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>M</em><sub>E</sub> is the total mass enclosed by the Einstein radius, <em>D</em><sub><em>LS</em></sub> is the distance from lens to source, <em>D</em><sub><em>L</em></sub> is the distance from observer to lens, and <em>D</em><sub><em>S</em></sub> is the distance from observer to source. All distances are angular diameter distances calculated from redshift. We emphasize that <em>M</em><sub>E</sub> does not denote the total mass of the galaxy but only the mass enclosed. Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) modeled and analyzed 73 SLACS lenses and presented a linear relation between the log of the total (lensing) mass enclosed within half the effective radius, a close match to the typical Einstein radius, and the log of stellar mass. Using the following relation</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{{\\rm{E}}}=0.0011{M}_{\\odot }{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{* }}{{M}_{\\odot }}\\right)}^{1.25}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>and utilizing stellar mass measurements from GAMA LAMBDAR stellar mass estimates (Taylor et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib71\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib71\">2011</a>), we estimate the total mass enclosed for use in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn1\">1</a>). Only the GAMA spectroscopy candidates include source redshift measurements; for these candidates, <em>D</em><sub><em>LS</em></sub> and <em>D</em><sub><em>S</em></sub> are calculated from this redshift. For LinKS machine-learning and GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates, the lens galaxy is assumed to be positioned approximately halfway between the observer and source, which is the distance at which the most dramatic strong lensing features should be observable. For comparison, we calculate a second Einstein radius estimate that accounts for mass distribution.</p></div><h4 id=\"ajabb612s3-3-2\" name=\"ajabb612s3-3-2\">3.3.2.\u00a0Singular Isothermal Sphere (Velocity Dispersion) Estimate</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Approximated as a singular isothermal sphere (SIS), the Einstein radius takes a new form:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}={\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{SIS}}}{186\\mathrm{km}{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}}\\right)}^{2}\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{{LS}}}{{D}_{S}}\\ {\\rm{arcsec}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>According to Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>), the <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub> parameter can be substituted with stellar velocity dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> as a first approximation. In order to obtain fiducial velocity dispersions, we adopt a combination of empirical <em>M</em><sub>*</sub><em>\u03c3</em> (total stellar mass to line-of-sight central stellar velocity dispersion) relations reported in Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>). For low-redshift galaxies, these relations are fits to measured data from ~370,000 objects with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9</script></span></span> and 0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.2 from SDSS DR 12 (Alam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib2\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib2\">2015</a>). The intermediate redshift relation is a fit to a sample of 4585 galaxies from the Smithsonian Hectoscopic Lens Survey (SHELS, Geller et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib40\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib40\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib39\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib39\">2014</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib38\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib38\">2016</a>). These relations take the form</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\sigma }_{{z}_{\\mathrm{low}}}={10}^{2.073}{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{M* }{{M}_{b}}\\right)}^{\\alpha }\\ \\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}\\quad ({\\rm{z}}\\lt 0.2,\\,{\\rm{SDSS}})\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn5.gif\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\sigma }_{{z}_{\\mathrm{int}}}={10}^{2.071}{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{M* }{{M}_{b}}\\right)}^{0.281}\\ \\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}\\quad (0.2\\leqslant {\\rm{z}}\\lt 0.65,\\,{\\rm{SHELS}})\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612ueqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ueqn1.gif\" alt=\"\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray*}\\begin{array}{rcl}{\\rm{where}}\\qquad \\alpha & = & 0.403\\qquad {\\rm{for}}\\qquad {M}_{* }\\leqslant {M}_{b}\\\\ \\alpha & = & 0.293\\qquad {\\rm{for}}\\qquad {M}_{* }\\gt {M}_{b}\\\\ {\\rm{and}}\\qquad {M}_{b} & = & {10}^{10.26}{M}_{\\odot }{\\rm{.}}\\end{array}\\end{eqnarray*}</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>These equations are applied to each candidate with reliable GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> mass estimates, and the resulting central stellar velocity dispersion estimate is taken to be <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> as an approximation to the parameter <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub> for use in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn3\">3</a>). As with the thin-lens estimate, <em>D</em><sub><em>LS</em></sub> and <em>D</em><sub><em>S</em></sub> are calculated from measured spectroscopic redshifts for GAMA spectroscopy candidates. For LinKS machine-learning and GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates, the ratio of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}</script></span></span> is assumed to be equal to two.</p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612s4\" name=\"ajabb612s4\">4.\u00a0Results</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We found remarkably little overlap between catalogs of candidates obtained by the three methods. Our final cuts of the three catalogs included no candidate common to all three methods and only two candidates common to two methods, both of which were between LinKS machine learning and GalaxyZoo citizen science, as shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f6\">6</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Venn diagram showing the number of lens candidates identified by each of the three methods following our final sample selections. Overlapping regions indicate the number of lens candidates identified by both (or all three) candidates, while numbers in each methods' region indicate the total number of candidates identified by that method. The two overlaps occurred between 47 LinKS machine-learning candidates and the 13 GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f6\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f6\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h3 id=\"ajabb612s4-1\" name=\"ajabb612s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Results of Einstein Radius Estimates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The Einstein radius is a fundamental feature of a lensing system that determines to a large degree the probability of detection by any lens-finding method (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib67\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib67\">2015</a>, and reference therein). Note that fiducial source redshifts are utilized in the estimation of Einstein radii for LinKS machine-learning and GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates, so comparisons to GAMA spectroscopy candidates and previously studied systems whose source redshifts are known should be considered with discretion. Shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f7\">7</a>, the third selection criterion for GalaxyZoo candidates, based on the capabilities of the instrument, removes several high scoring candidates from consideration. These removed objects are worth follow-up as candidates for galaxies showing tidal features. The results of the estimates applied to all three catalogs, displayed in the upper two plots of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f8\">8</a>, show that in general the thin-lens estimate is larger than the SIS estimate for the same candidates. This difference is further demonstrated in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f9\">9</a> and discussed in greater detail in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s6-1\">6.1</a>. Mean and median values for each sample for both models are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#ajabb612t2\">2</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0These figures show all GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates with \"Lens or arc\" scores higher than 30%. Top: histogram showing the distribution of Einstein radius estimates. Bottom: vertical axis shows \"Lens or arc\" score as percentage of votes. Candidates with estimated Einstein radii lower than the KiDS PSF are considered poor candidates and are removed from the final sample. The high scoring GalaxyZoo objects in this removed subsample are good candidates for tidal features. Note the overlapping candidates (LinKS\u2014GalaxyZoo) G93310 and G124486 are emphasized by the black square and diamond.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f7\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f7\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; Bottom: Einstein radius estimates of candidates identified by previous spectroscopic lens surveys for comparison. Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a> details calculations for the three GAMA/KiDS methods, while SIE estimates for SLACS, S4TM, and BELLS candidates are taken from Bolton et al. We take each of these to represent a range of probable values. Right: because thin-lens estimates are proportional to the inverse square of the lens distance, given a fixed ratio of source to lens distance (as we assume 2:1 for LinKS machine learning and GalaxyZoo citizen science), the thin-lens estimate decreases with redshift. As a result, the differences between estimates of the same candidates are pronounced at low redshift. This trend is further discussed in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s6-1-1\">6.1.1</a>\n</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block
