valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">G593852</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">218.0782</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\u22120.03958</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">GZ</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/suppdata/ajabb612t6_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-3881/abb612\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/suppdata/ajabb612t6_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>The surface shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f14\">14</a> reveals an interesting trend at stellar masses below <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>M</em><sub><em>b</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a010<sup>10.26</sup> and redshifts below <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2, which shows a steep rise with decreasing stellar mass due to the fit introduced in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn4\">4</a>) for lower mass and lower redshift. Only GAMA spectroscopy candidates, for which an assumed distance ratio was not applied, reside in this region. The few data points that appear to follow that trend cannot be considered to be direct evidence for it, since they were calculated from measured source redshifts. Of course there is the possibility of other redshift related effects in the estimation of Einstein radius and in how accurately these approximations determine the true Einstein radius of a system, but the redshift trend observed in the data as first shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f9\">9</a> appears to be a result of the chosen assumptions for GalaxyZoo and LinKS, which isolate the lens distance as a unique factor to one estimate (thin lens) and not the other (SIS). Importantly, this analysis is meant only to explore the limitations of the manner in which these estimates have been specifically applied to the candidates in this work so as to better understand how to properly consider the results of the application. The adopted fits from Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) and Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a> play a significant role in the algebraic relations discussed here.</p></div><h4 id=\"ajabb612s6-1-2\" name=\"ajabb612s6-1-2\">6.1.2.\u00a0Limitations of Adopted Empirical Fits</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We also consider more deeply the limitations of the application of these empirical fits to the estimation of Einstein radii for the candidates under consideration. We note that the adopted relation between <em>M</em><sub>E</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> (Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn2\">2</a>) in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a>) is calculated (1) from SLACS lenses with higher stellar masses than some of our candidates examined here, (2) within half the object's effective radius and not necessarily the Einstein radius, and (3) assuming a power-law profile for mass distribution. Through rigorous calculations, as detailed in Tortora et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib73\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib73\">2010</a>), Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>) show <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub> and thereby the calculated Einstein radius estimate to be higher than its stellar counterpart. This suggests that the Einstein radii calculated with the SIS model detailed above in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a> may be underestimates due to the approximation of <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> to <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub> in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn3\">3</a>). Conversely, the stellar velocity dispersion as presented in Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>) is measured within the SDSS and BOSS fibers (with 3'' and 2'' apertures, respectively), while the velocity dispersion in Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) has been corrected to a fiducial physical aperture of 3 kpc, which is progressively smaller in angular size at higher redshift. As a result, the <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> of Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) may be higher than the <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> used in Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib59\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib59\">2017</a>), which could account for the difference.</p><p>As an additional sanity check, we calculate the velocity dispersion by utilizing another fit from the SLACS lens analysis of Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>), which relates the velocity dispersion within half the effective radius to the total stellar mass of the galaxy. The relation can be written as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn9\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn9.gif\" alt=\"Equation (9)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\sigma }_{* }={10}^{2.34}{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{* }}{{10}^{11}{M}_{\\odot }}\\right)}^{0.18}\\ \\mathrm{km}\\,{{\\rm{s}}}^{-1}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>the result of which is shown in comparison to velocity dispersions derived from the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f15\">15</a>. Relative to the velocity dispersions derived from the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation, the Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) velocity dispersions are higher for candidates at lower stellar mass and lower for candidates at higher stellar mass. These differences result in greater differences in Einstein radius for candidates in those ranges. The difference between these two estimates may be due to the differences in parent samples. The Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation is based on a range of masses as well as accounting for different ranges of redshift (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9</script></span></span> from SDSS at <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.2, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn6.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9.5$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9.5</script></span></span> from SHELS at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.2), while the Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) relation is fit to a much smaller sample of SLACS lenses with a mean <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn7.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })</script></span></span> of 11.33, minimum 10.43, and maximum 11.79. This suggests that either prescription may be more appropriate for different selections depending on the mass ranges involved.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f15\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f15\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f15_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 15.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 15.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f15_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 15.</strong>\u00a0Velocity dispersions estimated from stellar mass of candidates derived from empirical fits taken from Auger et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>; squares) and from Zahid et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>; triangles). The differences that manifest in the lowest and highest ends of the stellar mass range result in greater differences in the estimated Einstein radii for those candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f15\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f15\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f15\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f15\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h4 id=\"ajabb612s6-1-3\" name=\"ajabb612s6-1-3\">6.1.3.\u00a0SLACS Lenses in GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> Catalog</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Two confirmed and well-studied SLACS grade-A lenses are present in the GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> catalog, which allows us to compare the three Einstein radius estimates to the measured Einstein radius of these two examples from the detailed modeling and analysis by Bolton et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib11\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib11\">2008a</a>). We estimate the Einstein radii for these two lenses utilizing both the true source redshifts and the assumed 2:1 ratio of source distance to lens distance that we apply to GAMA/KiDS candidates with unknown source redshifts. The resulting estimates are shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#ajabb612t5\">5</a> and serve to inform the GAMA spectroscopy estimates that include source redshifts as well as the LinKS and GalaxyZoo estimates that require the fiducial assumption. For G136604, which is one of the two overlaps between the LinKS machine-learning and GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidate catalogs, the true Einstein radius (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib11\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib11\">2008a</a>) lies between the SIS estimate from the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation and the thin-lens estimate, while the SIS estimate from Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) underestimates the true value by around half an arcsecond. The SIS estimate utilizing the true source redshift and the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation most closely approximates the true Einstein radius. In the case of G216398, it appears that the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) fit slightly overestimates the true Einstein radius, which does lie between the lowest SIS estimate (Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) and the thin-lens estimate. This may be a result of the fact that the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) velocity dispersion fit is less appropriate than the Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) fit for the highest mass galaxies, though again the Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) estimate is in fact the closest of the three models we have applied to the true value for both examples.</p><p>Based on the questions and insights discussed throughout this Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s6-1\">6.1</a>, we consider each estimate to inform the range of probable Einstein radius values. For the two SLACS examples, the SIS estimate approximates the measured value of the Einstein radius closely. However, given the low number statistics and the uncertainties introduced by adopting fiducial values for <em>\u03c3</em><sub>SIS</sub>, <em>M</em><sub>E</sub>, and <em>z</em><sub>source</sub>, we consider this far from conclusive. We elect to use the thin-lens estimate to pare down the GalaxyZoo selection as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s5\">5</a> because it is more inclusive and allows for occasional better KiDS PSF as well as GalaxyZoo selection improvement using color. We elect to use the <em>M</em><sub>*</sub><em>\u03c3</em> conversion from Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) instead of Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) because the former is fit to a larger sample that covers the entire range of masses occupied by our candidates objects. Given accurate source redshifts, each candidate could be modeled in a more complex manner to check these estimates more thoroughly. This follow-up validation, though worthwhile, is outside the scope of this paper. Deeper analysis and modeling of the other candidates presented in this paper will shed more light on the limitations of each of these models and assumptions. These first estimates of Einstein radii suffice to select the samples and plan for follow-up observations in a future study.</p></div></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s6-2\" name=\"ajabb612s6-2\">6.2.\u00a0LinKS Machine-learning Training Set</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Machine learning is an effective method for identifying image features similar to the training set utilized. Its effectiveness is then constrained inherently by the scope of its training set. The catalog analyzed here from Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>) used simulated images resembling SLACS lenses as their training set, and the candidates it identified are characteristically similar in terms of estimated Einstein radius, stellar mass, and redshift to those identified by SLACS spectroscopy. With a training sample volumetrically skewed toward massive elliptical galaxies (the early-type galaxies sample in KiDS) with large Einstein radii, the resulting identified candidates are equally biased toward higher masses and Einstein radii, as shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f8\">8</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f16\">16</a>. This bias can be amplified through the problem of transfer learning.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f16\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f16\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f16_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 16.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 16.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f16_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 16.</strong>\u00a0High scoring LinKS machine-learning candidates show estimated Einstein radii above the minimum (1'') utilized for the training set. The highest scoring LinKS candidates appear to have the largest Einstein radii, due to the maximal separation of lensing features from the image of the foreground galaxy. GalaxyZoo candidates do not appear to show this same trend, though the size of the candidate sample makes generalization difficult.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f16\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f16\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f16\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f16\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s6-3\" name=\"ajabb612s6-3\">6.3.\u00a0Fiber Radius Mass and GAMA Spectroscopic Identification</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Lens candidate identification through any spectroscopic method requires sufficient flux from the background galaxy in order to obtain a second spectral match. For lensing systems whose Einstein radius exceeds the radius of the instrument's aperture, the probability of detection goes down significantly and rapidly (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib67\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib67\">2015</a>, and reference therein). For GAMA spectroscopy we therefore introduce a characteristic mass that we call the <em>fiber radius mass</em> and define it as follows: <em>M</em><sub>fr</sub> is the precise total stellar mass of a lensing system (of which the lens and source redshift are known) for which the estimated Einstein radius equals the radius of the spectroscopic fiber, assuming the fiber has been positioned precisely at the center of the lensing object, i.e., <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>(<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>fr</sub>)\u00a0=\u00a01''. For GAMA spectroscopy, this represents a soft upper constraint on the total stellar mass that corresponds to a total enclosed mass <em>M</em><sub>E</sub> or stellar velocity dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub>*</sub> that will contribute to an Einstein radius that will fit within the instrument's aperture.</p><p>Taking into account GAMA spectroscopy's 1'' radius aperture, we derive expressions for stellar mass that correspond to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01'' using the equations of Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a>. The left plot of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f17\">17</a> shows colored curves parameterized by the ratio of source distance to lens distance that show the stellar mass and lens candidate redshift which result in thin-lens Einstein radius estimates equal to the fiber radius of 1''. This relation of lens distance to stellar mass is taken from Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn1\">1</a>) and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn2\">2</a>) and is shown for various ratios of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn8.gif\" alt=\"$A=\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">A=\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}</script></span></span> represented by the colored curves</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn10\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn10.gif\" alt=\"Equation (10)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{L}}{\\mathrm{Mpc}}=\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{A-1}{A}\\right)\\left(0.011\\times {10}^{-8.09}\\right){\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{M}_{* }}{M\\odot }\\right)}^{1.25}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>Redshift is easily converted from angular diameter distance.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f17\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f17\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f17_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 17.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 17.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f17_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 17.</strong>\u00a0The data presented here is the same as in Figure <a href=\"#ajabb612f10\">10</a> with additional elements. These elements are introduced to represent total stellar mass equal to the fiber radius mass (<em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>fr</sub>), which results in an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01'' (the aperture size of GAMA spectroscopic fibers). Left: curves parameterized by the ratio of source distance to lens distance show the total stellar mass and lens candidate redshift where total stellar mass is equal to the fiber radius mass. Right: vertical lines reflect the general independence of SIS velocity dispersion Einstein radius estimates (Equation (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn3\">3</a>)) on lens redshift, with the exception of the <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.2 break in the fit of stellar velocity dispersions to stellar mass (Equations (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn4\">4</a>) and (<a class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn5\">5</a>)) that results in the horizontal break at this redshift in the plot. For both plots, data points that fall along a given curve or line will have a 1'' Einstein radius for the given ratio of source distance to lens distance. Note that unknown source redshifts for LinKS and GalaxyZoo candidates could also affect the Einstein radius estimate, were they available. For this reason, we have shown the fiber radius mass curves and lines for a variety of source to lens distance ratios. Multiple candidates identified by the other two methods have stellar masses that exceed the GAMA spectroscopy fiber radius mass at the given lens candidate redshift.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f17\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f17\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>For SIS models that estimate <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> from velocity dispersion, the Einstein radius is not inherently proportional to the lens candidate distance, but instead to the ratio of the distances of the lens and source. We use Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn3\">3</a>), (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn4\">4</a>), and (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#ajabb612eqn5\">5</a>) to derive the total stellar mass that corresponds to <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01'' for these models</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn11\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn11.gif\" alt=\"Equation (11)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{* }={\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{A-1}{A}\\right)}^{\\tfrac{-1}{0.586}}(8.52\\times {10}^{10})\\quad z\\lt 0.2\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"ajabb612eqn12\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612eqn12.gif\" alt=\"Equation (12)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{* }={\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{A-1}{A}\\right)}^{\\tfrac{-1}{0.562}}(9.26\\times {10}^{10})\\quad z\\geqslant 0.2.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>This results in lines of constant stellar mass at which, for the given redshift range and ratio <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn9.gif\" alt=\"$A=\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">A=\\tfrac{{D}_{S}}{{D}_{L}}</script></span></span>, <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>fr</sub> and the Einstein radius is 1''.</p><p>For a given ratio of source distance to lens distance, <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> estimates of candidates that fall to the right of the associated curve will exceed the radius of the GAMA spectroscopy fiber (1''). Multiple candidates from the other two methods have stellar masses exceeding the curves for given ratios <em>A</em>. This indicates that GAMA spectroscopy may have little chance of observing enough flux from the background source galaxy to acquire the second redshift match required for identification. It is important to note that this is not a hard limit on the <em>possibility</em> of lens candidate identification. The geometry and surface brightness of the features rarely resemble the perfect Einstein ring whose features lie exactly along the Einstein radius. These features extend, stretch, and become asymmetric across the center of the galaxy and can result in sufficient flux for background source detection. In addition, the placement of the aperture is not always precisely centered on the object. These possibilities in addition to chance alignment can and do allow the detection of lensing objects with Einstein radii larger than the fiber. However, as we have previously stated, the probability of detection diminishes quickly when the Einstein radius extends beyond the fiber radius.</p></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s6-4\" name=\"ajabb612s6-4\">6.4.\u00a0Low-mass Lensing Galaxies</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>GalaxyZoo citizen science and GAMA spectroscopy found an encouraging number of candidates at lower masses than those identified through the LinKS machine-learning method considered here, as well as many previous lens studies that focused on galaxies fitting the description of SDSS LRGs (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib34\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib34\">2001</a>). These candidates could potentially be analyzed in order to study the structure of galaxies and their dark matter content in this lower range of galaxy mass. However, each arc from GalaxyZoo will need to be confirmed spectroscopically before modeling these as lensing systems, and more samples are needed in order to study this parameter space with meaningful results.</p></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s6-5\" name=\"ajabb612s6-5\">6.5.\u00a0Candidates and LRG Color\u2013Magnitude Selection</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The color\u2013magnitude selection criteria for SDSS LRGs detailed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s5\">5</a> were applied (with the empirical factor set to 1 to represent the most lenient selection) to GAMA Lambdar AB photometry of the final candidate samples of GAMA spectroscopy, LinKS machine-learning, and GalaxyZoo citizen-science methods.</p><p>Only three of the 47 GAMA spectroscopy candidates pass the LRG cuts. All but one candidate have a magnitude in the <em>r</em>-band &lt; 20. Forty-three candidates fail the <em>c</em><sub>par</sub> color parameter criterion, which represents a sliding luminosity threshold as a function of redshift, by a mean of 1.3\u202fmag and median of 1.2 mag. This could possibly indicate that there are more strong lenses to be found by broadening the search beyond the assumption of LRG dominance, a result also found in Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>).</p><p>Thirty-nine of the 47 LinKS machine-learning candidates pass the LRG cuts. Three are at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.4, and therefore do not follow the criteria. The remaining five fail the criterion relating to the <em>c</em><sub>par</sub> color parameter. These candidates all lie in the redshift range <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.25\u22120.3 and fail the color selection criterion by a mean of 0.47\u202fmag and median of 0.26 mag. Eisenstein et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib34\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib34\">2001</a>) points out that the LRG color\u2013magnitude selection becomes less appropriate as object redshift decreases well below <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.4. One of these candidates is G124486, one of the candidates that overlaps with the GalaxyZoo sample at a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.144. Note that these 47 candidates were all originally selected using the same color\u2013magnitude criteria applied to a different set of photometric measurements.</p><p>Eight of 13 GalaxyZoo citizen-science candidates pass the LRG cuts. The five remaining candidates fail the <em>c</em><sub>par</sub> color parameter criterion by a mean of 0.21\u202fmag and median of 0.11 mag. As mentioned above, these LRG cuts are not well suited to the redshift range of GalaxyZoo candidates. This could possibly indicate that they are less luminous or less red, further evidence of viable strong lens candidates outside the realm of the LRG assumption. Alternatively, these candidates may pass LRG cuts that are adjusted to be more appropriate to the redshift range. Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>) makes no mention of any adjustment to color\u2013magnitude selection criteria for galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.15.</p></div><h3 id=\"ajabb612s6-6\" name=\"ajabb612s6-6\">6.6.\u00a0Other Lens Searches and Future Efforts</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Similar efforts, mostly machine learning, have found strong lens candidates in deep imaging surveys (e.g., Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib47\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib47\">2019</a>, Speagle et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib68\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib68\">2019</a>, and Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib46\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib46\">2020b</a>, in HSC, DECaLS, and DES, respectively). Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f10\">10</a> shows the GAMA equatorial lens candidates of this work and includes two candidates previously identified by DECaLS, SLACS (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib11\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib11\">2008a</a>) in the GAMA equatorial regions that had a match in R.A./decl. to the GAMA catalog. Both of these candidates were identified by GalaxyZoo citizen science, and one was also identified by LinKS machine learning. This lends confidence to GalaxyZoo's ability to return high quality lens candidates. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f18\">18</a> shows the numbers of identified lens candidates per unit of sky against the survey characteristics. The majority of theses studies aimed for a clean (reliable but incomplete) rather than complete sample of galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses. Our results show that the combination of methods implies a much higher sky density of lenses in a given survey. Key for spectroscopic survey identification is completeness, a key driver of the GAMA survey (Driver et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib33\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib33\">2009</a>; Robotham et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib63\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib63\">2010</a>; Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib7\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib7\">2012</a>), and an automated redshift finder (Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib6\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib6\">2014</a>). For imaging surveys, the key drivers are spatial resolution (subarcsecond seeing) and survey depth. The on-sky density of strong lenses is critical if one wants to estimate, for example, the rates of strongly lensed events such as supernovae (B. W. Holwerda et al. 2020, in preparation).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f18\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f18\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f18_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 18.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 18.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f18_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 18.</strong>\u00a0A comparison between the different identifications of strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates in different surveys, using blended spectroscopy (left) and deep imaging (right). The left figure shows SLACS (Bolton et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib12\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib12\">2006</a>), S4TM (Shu et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib66\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib66\">2017</a>) from SDSS-DR7 (Abazajian et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib1\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib1\">2009</a>), and BELLS (Brownstein et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib16\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib16\">2012</a>) spectroscopic searches compared to the GAMA spectroscopy candidates selected in this work from Holwerda et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib42\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib42\">2015</a>). The right figure shows the LinKS survey from Petrillo et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>) and the candidates presented in this paper from GAMA/KiDS GalaxyZoo citizen science, compared with deep imaging searches in DES (Jacobs et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib47\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib47\">2019</a>) and DECaLS (Huang et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib46\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib46\">2020b</a>). We note that SDSS spectroscopic searches aimed for clean rather than complete samples (requiring high signal-to-noise ratios) and GAMA emphasizes completeness throughout the survey. S4TM and SLACS report the number of observed candidates for an HST Snapshot program, which implies a longer candidate list. The candidate number per unit area is therefore a lower limit.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f18\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f18\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h4 id=\"ajabb612s6-6-1\" name=\"ajabb612s6-6-1\">6.6.1.\u00a0Li et al. (2020) Machine-learning \"Bright Galaxy\" Candidates</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>During the preparation of this paper, Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>) published the results of their CNN's selection of strong lens candidates in the KiDS DR4 data using a technique similar to, though slightly modified from, the LinKS machine-learning method. They offer qualitative comparison to Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib60\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib60\">2019a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib61\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib61\">2019b</a>), noting that their application of the CNN to an expanded sample of \"bright galaxies\" (BGs) without LRG cuts doubled the number of quality lens candidates identified by their algorithm. Their result supports this work's assertion that the expansion of machine learning's scope beyond the standard assumption of LRGs will improve the completeness of such automated lens-finding methods. Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>) further note their intention to follow with more quantitative comparison with the LinKS candidates in a future paper, and also comment on the value of comparison with other lens-finding methods. Forty-eight of their BG candidates fall within the 180 deg<sup>2</sup> considered in this study and have a match by R.A./decl. to an object in GAMA. Thirty-nine of these have a reliable stellar mass estimate from GAMA <span class=\"small-caps\">LAMBDAR</span> photometry, and we apply the same procedure for Einstein radius estimation outlined in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a>. The BG candidates of Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>) are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f19\">19</a> in comparison to the candidates considered in this study. They tend to lie within a similar region of parameter space as the LinKS machine-learning candidates we have considered in this paper, with <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.2\u22120.5 (mean and median 0.33) and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn10.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\sim 11\\mbox{--}12$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\sim 11\\mbox{--}12</script></span></span> (mean 11.55, median 11.61). These candidates do not overlap with any candidates identified here. The follow-up study of those data in comparison with the data from this study in more detail is worth conducting.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f19\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f19\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f19_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 19.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 19.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f19_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 19.</strong>\u00a0Top: thin-lens and SIS Einstein radius estimates for \"Bright Galaxy\" candidates from Li et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>) in comparison with the three methods examined in this study. Bottom: stellar mass\u2013redshift space for the same candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f19\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f19\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f19\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f19\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h4 id=\"ajabb612s6-6-2\" name=\"ajabb612s6-6-2\">6.6.2.\u00a0Hubble and Future Surveys</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The Hubble Legacy Archive (HLA; Whitmore et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib78\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib78\">2016</a>) contains imaging information on four of the lensing candidates presented here in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#ajabb612t6\">6</a> and Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f20\">20</a>. G136604 was identified in both the LinKS machine-learning and GalaxyZoo citizen-science selections (KiDS cutout shown also in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f12\">12</a>, left) and was a known strong lens from the SLACS survey (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib15\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib15\">2008b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib14\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib14\">2012</a>; Auger et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib9\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib9\">2011</a>; Czoske et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib26\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib26\">2012</a>; Shu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib65\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib65\">2015</a>). The second example, G3882191, was identified as a strong lensing galaxy group in SL2S (Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib53\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib53\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib54\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib54\">2010</a>) and has been recovered by LinKS machine learning. Because G3882191 is located at the center of a massive dark matter halo associated with a galaxy group, it has a particularly large measured Einstein radius as a result (5<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />48). This is much greater than the estimates we compute from GAMA data (~2'') using the adopted empirical fits to the lensing parameters as described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#ajabb612s3-3\">3.3</a>. This perhaps exposes a limitation to the analysis we have applied to these candidates if they are located at the center of a much larger lensing galaxy group. On the other hand, the LinKS method has correctly identified this as a lensing system, showing that the selection based on Einstein radius is still valid for this candidate. Follow-up could examine more closely the role of environment in strong lensing systems, but this consideration is outside the scope of this paper. G204703 is part of a cluster, and the arc is partially the result of cluster strong lensing (Zitrin &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib83\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib83\">2016</a>; Dessauges-Zavadsky et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib31\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib31\">2017</a>; Zitrin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib82\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib82\">2017</a>; Newman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib58\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib58\">2018</a>). One of the GalaxyZoo identifications, G593852, is in the HLA, but the arc is faint in the Hubble single filter image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f20\">20</a>). These images illustrate that these two selection methods find valid strong lens candidates and show that Hubble images on all lens candidates could validate their nature and make detailed lensing models possible.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f20\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f20\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f20_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 20.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 20.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f20_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 20.</strong>\u00a0Examples of lensing candidates found in the Hubble Legacy Archive. Grayscale image in F814W, except G593852, which is in F625W. F625W is significantly narrower than F814W, which results in the lower contrast of the image of G593852. G593852 and G204703 are presented next to their corresponding KiDS cutout images. G136604 was identified by SLACS, LinKS machine learning, and GalaxyZoo citizen science. The arc shown next to G204703 may be due to cluster lensing. G3882191 was previously identified as a strong lensing galaxy group in SL2S (Limousin et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib53\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib53\">2009</a>, <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib54\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib54\">2010</a>) and is recovered by LinKS machine learning.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f20\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f20\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f20\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f20\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>With improved spatial resolution for wide-field imaging (WFIRST and EUCLID), the machine-learning and citizen-science identification will overlap more in mass and redshift with the spectroscopic identifications of strong lens candidates. Wide-field integral field spectroscopy (e.g., SAMI, MUSE, and 4MOST WAVES wide surveys) could potentially identify strong lenses from a mix of spectroscopic and imaging information (curved arcs in the data cube). Future large telescopes (GMT, ELT, and TMT) could follow up the higher redshift or lower mass lens candidates, and the WEAVE and 4MOST instruments are set to select or confirm many candidates spectroscopically.</p><p>For future identification of galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates (e.g., LSST or WFIRST imaging), a hybrid approach between citizen science and machine learning (see Beck et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib10\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib10\">2018</a>) could result in a more complete and reliable yield of strong lensing candidates. This is in our view a better approach than to try to validate galaxy\u2013galaxy lens candidates using different methods. A worthwhile next step will be to design and implement a CNN with a more complete training set using our findings and the work of Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib61\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib61\">2019b</a>) and Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>) to experiment with its application to KiDS imaging and HST archival data and to prepare for future wide-field imaging surveys. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f21\">21</a> shows that several of the candidates unidentified by LinKS machine learning could be recovered in these deeper surveys that will be able to resolve the features of lenses with even the smallest of estimated Einstein radii from this study. We believe the deliberate expansion of the training set to include lenses with color features beyond the LRG assumption will improve the completeness a step further than that achieved by Li et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib52\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib52\">2020</a>). Detection and modeling of these smaller lenses may reveal insights into dark matter fractions in the lower mass regime. Alternatively, an entirely new selection technique could result in a selection of similar completeness as the three presented here.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f21\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f21\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f21_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 21.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 21.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f21_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 21.</strong>\u00a0The data presented here is the same as in Figure <a href=\"#ajabb612f10\">10</a> with marker size corresponding to estimated Einstein radius. The largest of estimated Einstein radii tend to be at higher mass and lower lens redshift. The gray reference marker indicates the benchmark of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01'' (corresponding to the aperture size of GAMA spectroscopic fibers). Next generation surveys (JWST, Euclid, WFIRST, and LSST) will have optical resolutions comparable to HST, on the order of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1\u20130<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2. In theory, under the right conditions, these surveys should be able to resolve features from even some of the smallest estimated Einstein radii considered in this survey.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f21\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f21\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f21\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f21\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612s7\" name=\"ajabb612s7\">7.\u00a0Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Our analysis of the data generated by the three methods leads us to the following conclusions regarding the biases and advantages of the methods as well as the relation of each to the others:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Each of the three specific methods analyzed here is an ineffective means of vetting candidate samples obtained by either of the other two methods due to the lack of overlap between candidate properties, which we illustrate in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f6\">6</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f10\">10</a>.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">The differences in candidate properties arise primarily from selection functions inherent to the parent sample selections and procedures of each method; specifically:<ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(a)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Blended spectra identifies only those candidate galaxies whose lens features contribute significant flux within the radius of the fiber aperture collecting the spectrum. GAMA's depth and completeness extend candidate selection to a lower mass range than those identified by previous strong lens surveys, including those conducted within SDSS. A wider aperture or integral field spectroscopy (e.g., SAMI or MUSE) would allow the possibility of identifying lower redshift or more massive galaxies than the GAMA spectroscopy candidates examined here.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(b)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Machine learning finds candidates with similar features to its training sets, i.e., a well-separated lens and arc. This limitation can be improved upon with higher resolution images and a wider variation in training sets, including lens galaxies that do not conform to LRG characteristics.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">(c)\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">GalaxyZoo's upper threshold on redshift limits its range of applicability, and the wide focus of its classification stage introduces significant challenges to candidate quality assessment. Higher resolution images and a higher redshift cutoff together would allow for the inclusion of more distant galaxies, e.g., for WFIRST, LSST, or Euclid citizen-science efforts.</div></li></ul>\n</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Machine learning has the promise to be the most efficient automated lens identification technique, and substantial effort must be made to improve these algorithms ahead of the next generation of galaxy surveys.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">In the meantime, other methods\u2014including blended spectra and citizen science\u2014remain useful for extending and diversifying the catalog of lens candidates available for study and for their application in the training of machine-learning algorithms.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>Strong gravitational lenses offer a unique laboratory to be used in cosmology measures and the next level of accuracy in estimates of dark matter distribution and substructure. The much greater samples needed (orders of magnitude higher than the current number of identified lenses) require automated identification. Using a combined systematic approach to improve the scope of machine learning's applicability, a more complete census of these rare objects can be achieved for the next generation of imaging surveys (e.g., WFIRST, Euclid, and LSST).</p></div>\n<div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>S. Knabel acknowledges the support of the Summer Research Opportunity Program (SROP) and the Undergraduate Research Grant (URG) by the University of Louisville's Office of the Executive Vice President for Research and Innovation (EVPRI). The material is based upon work supported by NASA Kentucky under NASA award no: NNX15AR69H (R. Steele). This work was supported by a NASA Keck PI Data Award, administered by the NASA Exoplanet Science Institute.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"ajabb612app1\" name=\"ajabb612app1\">Appendix</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"ajabb612app1-1\" name=\"ajabb612app1-1\">A.1.\u00a0Alternate Cuts</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The final cuts were intended to ensure a comparable and reliable catalog of lens candidates for each method analyzed. The numbers reflected in our final catalogs will vary if considered under different cuts, so we also examined the overlap considering a more relaxed cut that included all GAMA spectroscopy candidates, all LinKS machine-learning candidates with scores greater than 0, and all GalaxyZoo candidates with lens scores greater than 20%. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#ajabb612f22\">22</a> shows that this cut improves overlap to a small extent. However, it introduces a significant increase in false positives and unreliable candidates, which we considered to outweigh the benefits of the improvement to overlap for the purposes of this study. The overlaps that we see in the more relaxed cuts do appear in the expected parameter space, with masses and redshifts comparable to the two overlap candidates that appear in our final catalogs.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"ajabb612f22\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"ajabb612f22\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f22_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 22.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 22.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612f22_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 22.</strong>\u00a0Venn diagram showing the number of lens candidates identified by each of the three methods with more lenient cuts to both GalaxyZoo citizen science and LinKS machine learning. Candidates with \"Lens or arc\" scores above 20% and LinKS machine-learning candidates with lens scores greater than 0 are included in this selection. Overlapping regions indicate the number of lens candidates identified by both (or all three) candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-ajabb612f22\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadFigure/figure/ajabb612f22\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-ajabb612f22\" href=\"/1538-3881/160/5/223/downloadHRFigure/figure/ajabb612f22\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) velocity dispersions are higher for candidates at lower stellar mass and lower for candidates at higher stellar mass. These differences result in greater differences in Einstein radius for candidates in those ranges. The difference between these two estimates may be due to the differences in parent samples. The Zahid et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib81\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib81\">2016</a>) relation is based on a range of masses as well as accounting for different ranges of redshift (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9</script></span></span> from SDSS at <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.2, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn6.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9.5$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })\\gt 9.5</script></span></span> from SHELS at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.2), while the Auger et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#ajabb612bib5\" id=\"fnref-ajabb612bib5\">2010</a>) relation is fit to a much smaller sample of SLACS lenses with a mean <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/1538-3881/160/5/223/Full/ajabb612ieqn7.gif\" alt=\"$\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\mathrm{log}({M}_{* }/{M}_{\\odot })</script></span></span> of 11.33, minimum 10.43, and maximum 11.79. I.", "Brough, Sarah", "Kelvin, Lee", "Bilicki, Maciej", "Kielkopf, John"], "author_address": ["AA(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA), AB(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA), AC(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA), AD(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA), AE(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA), AF(Australian Astronomical Optics, Macquarie University, 105 Delhi Road, North Ryde, NSW 2113, Australia), AG(School of Physics &amp; Astronomy, University of Nottingham, University Park, Nottingham, NG7 2RD, UK), AH(School of Physics &amp; Astronomy, Monash University, Clayton, VIC 3800, Australia), AI(School of Physics, University of New South Wales, NSW 2052, Australia), AJ(Department of Astrophysical Sciences, Princeton University, 4 Ivy Lane, Princeton, NJ 08544, USA), AK(Center for Theoretical Physics, Polish Academy of Sciences, Warsaw, Poland), AL(Department of Physics and Astronomy, 102 Natural Science Building, University of Louisville, Louisville KY 40292, USA)"], "journal_name": "The Astronomical Journal", "volume": "160", "publication_year": "2020/11/1", "start_page": "223", "keywords": ["Giant elliptical galaxies", "Strong gravitational lensing", "Redshift surveys", "Galaxy dark matter halos", "651", "1643", "1378", "1880", "Astrophysics - Astrophysics of Galaxies"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2020AJ....160..223K", "abstract": "Strong gravitational lenses are a rare and instructive type of astronomical object. Identification has long relied on serendipity, but different strategies\u2014such as mixed spectroscopy of multiple galaxies along the line of sight, machine-learning algorithms, and citizen science\u2014have been employed to identify these objects as new imaging surveys become available. We report on the comparison between spectroscopic, machine-learning, and citizen-science identification of galaxy-galaxy lens candidates from independently constructed lens catalogs in the common survey area of the equatorial fields of the Galaxy and Mass Assembly survey. In these, we have the opportunity to compare high completeness spectroscopic identifications against high-fidelity imaging from the Kilo Degree Survey used for both machine-learning and citizen-science lens searches. We find that the three methods\u2014spectroscopy, machine learning, and citizen science\u2014identify 47, 47, and 13 candidates, respectively, in the 180 square degrees surveyed. These identifications barely overlap, with only two identified by both citizen science and machine learning. We have traced this discrepancy to inherent differences in the selection functions of each of the three methods, either within their parent samples (i.e., citizen science focuses on low redshift) or inherent to the method (i.e., machine learning is limited by its training sample and prefers well-separated features, while spectroscopy requires sufficient flux from lensed features to lie within the fiber). These differences manifest as separate samples in estimated Einstein radius, lens stellar mass, and lens redshift. The combined sample implies a lens candidate sky density of \u223c0.59 deg<SUP>-2</SUP> and can inform the construction of a training set spanning a wider mass-redshift space. A combined approach and refinement of automated searches would result in a more complete sample of galaxy-galaxy lens candidates for future surveys.", "doi": "10.3847/1538-3881/abb612", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:2009.09493", "issn": "0004-6256", "number": "5"}}
